Are there any signs that we here in Britain will soon move into a mood of active lobbying at the United Nations for a global ban on arms and an investment embargo against Burma? The Karen, Karenni and Shan peoples are looking to the free world to give them some hope for the future. It is worth noting that the State Peace and Development Council spends an estimated 60 per cent of the national budget on the military, much of it on weapons from China—expenditure that is used to control and kill its own people.

There are many parts of our troubled world where human rights abuses regularly take place. In many places, the abuse is perpetrated by so-called democratically-elected governments and military force. It is a sad but true fact that bullies who govern by force and torture come to understand that nations such as ours are serious about bringing about change only when their own economies and military power are endangered by properly enforced economic sanctions.

4.15 pm

Baroness Cox: I warmly congratulate my noble friend Lord Alton on securing this opportunity to bring once again the tragic situation in Burma to the attention of your Lordships' House and to give the Minister the opportunity to explain Her Majesty's Government's response to the crimes against humanity and attempted genocide being perpetrated by the SPDC regime against ethnic national groups. I also congratulate my noble friend on his persistent endeavours to raise awareness of the dire situation in Burma and to urge the international community to do more to bring an end to the atrocities being perpetrated by the SPDC regime. I know that my noble friend is much loved and appreciated by those whose plight we consider today.

I do not use phrases such as "crimes against humanity" and "attempted genocide" without evidence to justify their appropriateness. My noble friend has powerfully described the background: Burma is ruled by a military junta with that Orwellian name "State Peace and Development Council". It seized power from the democratically elected government and oppresses all who oppose it. It has been systematically attacking the ethnic national peoples such as the Karen, Karenni and Shan, and the Mon, Chin, Kachin and Arakan peoples. Its policies include offences against innocent civilians; compelling civilians to undertake forced labour in conditions so harsh that many perish; the use of civilians as human minesweepers; systematic rape, torture and murder; the destruction of villages and the use of landmines to prevent villagers from returning to collect belongings or harvest their crops; and the abduction of boys for conscription into the army.
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC15
 

Hundreds of thousands of civilians have been forced to flee across the borders into Thailand to the east or to India in the west in order to survive. Thousands more are hiding in the jungles as internally displaced people with no adequate food, shelter or healthcare. Many die from disease and malnutrition. If found by SPDC troops, they face a fate often worse than death, as we heard from my noble friend, so they exist in a state of constant fear, ready to flee for their lives at a moment's notice if SPDC troops approach their hiding places.

There is now a massive collection of authoritative, accurate evidence documenting the atrocities perpetrated by the SPDC. My noble friend gave some examples, such as Guy Horton's compelling study. I will refer to a few brief other examples of well documented evidence.

Burma has the highest number of child soldiers in the world, 70,000, according to the Human Rights Watch report My Gun Was As Tall As Me. I have interviewed some of them. Some children come from the ethnic national groups whose plight we consider today. I have spoken to some of the young boys who managed to escape and their stories are heartbreaking. These boy soldiers, little more than terrified schoolchildren, describe how they were kidnapped and had no contact with their parents, so their families had no idea where they were. Conditions in the army are harsh and they abhor some of the tasks inflicted on them. One boy who escaped told me that he risked his life escaping because he could not endure being ordered to beat elderly people who had been forced to work as porters by SPDC soldiers and who collapsed having to carry 60 pounds of rice or ammunition from dawn to dusk, with hardly any food or water.

The systematic use of rape as a weapon of war has been documented in numerous reports, including the Shan Women's Action Networks Licence to Rape, the Karen Women's Organization report Shattering Silences and, as recently as this year, Catwalk to the Barracks: conscription of women for sexual slavery and other practices of sexual violence by troops of the Burmese regime in Mon Areas. The detailed accounts are indescribably heartbreaking.

The widespread and systematic use of forced labour continues and has been documented by many organisations, including the International Labour Organisation. The report to which my noble friend has already referred, Guy Horton's Dying Alive, gives a lot of formidable statistics, which I will not repeat.

Those are some of the bases of evidence and they include many compelling statistics. However, I should like to focus on one or two first-hand examples of human stories behind the statistics. They are based on a visit I made only last month to the Karen, Karenni and Shan peoples and on a previous visit to the Chin peoples from north-western Burma and the Kachin peoples. I am aware that the Question focuses on the Karen, Karenni and the Shan peoples, but I hope that I will be forgiven if I also mention the other ethnic national groups, such as the Chin, because they are
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC16
 
also suffering at the hands of this regime. Any answer which the Minister can give relating to some of the ethnic groups will have relevance for all.

I should like to give one or two human examples. We asked the Karenni children in one of the orphanages we support if they would be willing to draw pictures of their experiences inside Burma which had forced them to flee from their homeland. Graphic images of the children's memories of seeing the execution of their families bespeak a suffering beyond words.

My second example relates to a woman aged 29. I met her just a month ago. She described how she and her four children had to leave Mawchi township in Karenni state because SPDC soldiers repeatedly forced them to serve as porters. Her husband, aged 29, had been used as a human minesweeper. He had been forced to walk ahead of SPDC soldiers as a porter, but he was killed by a mine. She was pregnant, and her younger daughter was born after her husband was killed. Her eldest son was beaten by SPDC soldiers; he now suffers from permanent brain damage. That is a tragedy encapsulated in just one family.

We also crossed into Burma and visited IDPs living in conditions of severe deprivation. We were met by a very dedicated school teacher who described how he had run out of food for the children in his school on the day of our arrival. The only other NGO to visit his community that year had come several months earlier. With the money it had given, the community bought rice and baby fish, building a reservoir for the fish to grow to maturity. The previous day, their rice supplies had finished, but the fish were fully grown and they were going to have a "fish feast". But that night there had been a tropical storm, and a flash flood had washed away the walls of the little reservoir—and all the fish. The school teacher had been agonising over the problem of finding food for the children—until we arrived.

My fourth example is a young mother, whose five children had died of malaria a few weeks before I met her.

My final point refers to the issue raised by my noble friend Lord Alton on the allegations of the use of chemical weapons. We had the opportunity to interview three young men, who had been near a shell as it exploded, which had emitted a very unusual gas. Their symptoms then, at the time the shell exploded and now, quite a long time afterwards, are entirely consistent with the possibility of exposure to substances used in chemical weapons. I join my noble friend in urging Her Majesty's Government to press the international community to initiate appropriate investigations.

These five vignettes illustrate the suffering inflicted on the Karen, Karenni and Shan people of Burma. They also illustrate the effects of the regime's refusal to allow access by humanitarian aid organisations and human rights organisations to those whom it is victimising. So hundreds of thousands are living in fear of being caught and killed, and are dying from exposure, treatable disease and starvation.
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC17
 

Before I conclude, I should like to mention the plight of the other ethnic groups, such as the Chin people in north-western Burma. They have also suffered from violations of human rights, such as expropriation of land and subjection to forced labour. As a predominantly Christian people, they have experienced religious persecution, with the systematic destruction of churches and the crosses they build at crossroads and on hilltops. Some have been forced to contribute to the replacement of their churches by pagodas.

Many have had to flee into neighbouring India to survive, living in dire conditions in rural areas or urban so-called camps in cities. We found those living in the countryside suffering from lack of food, medicine and any prospects of developing sustainable means for survival, while those in the urban camps were existing 50 to a room with no adequate facilities or provisions of any kind.

I echo and endorse my noble friend's conclusion that incontrovertible evidence proves that the SPDC regime in Burma is guilty of long-standing, systematic crimes against humanity and attempted genocide. In Thailand last month, I was privileged to meet a group of Thai senators who were members of the ASEAN Inter-Parliamentary Myanmar Caucus, bravely speaking out for democracy in Burma. They have been actively calling for Burma to be on the UN Security Council agenda. I join my noble friend Lord Alton in urging Her Majesty's Government actively to support such an initiative.

Will Her Majesty's Government and their EU partners press the SPDC regime to prohibit all human rights violations, including murder, torture and rape; the use of forced labour and child soldiers; and, most importantly, to allow human rights and humanitarian organisations free access to all parts of Burma? If not we will, as my noble friend said, be guilty not only of betraying a defenceless people who are suffering and dying while we talk here today, but we will be doubly culpable. These are people who have paid a high price for supporting us in the Second World War. They gave their lives, alongside our soldiers, in the battle for freedom then. Will we leave them to suffer and die without any effective support from us now? They are looking to us for help in this their hour of desperate need. I hope that they will not look in vain this afternoon.

Lord Chan: My Lords, I, too, congratulate and thank my noble friend Lord Alton on securing this debate. I apologise to him for missing his opening remarks. 28 Nov 2005 : Column GC18
 
widespread and systematic use of rape; forced labour; forced relocation of villages; forced conscription of child soldiers; use of human minesweepers; torture; extrajudicial killings; and the destruction of villages, crops and livestock.

Evidence has been documented by international organisations, including the US Government, the United Nations and human rights NGOs such as Amnesty International, Human Rights Watch, Christian Solidarity Worldwide and the Jubilee Campaign. The political, economic and humanitarian situation in Burma is deteriorating rapidly. One Karen refugee said in August this year, "The situation is getting worse by the day. If people stay in Burma, how can they get rice?".

In addition to the economic crisis in Burma, at least 1 million people are internally displaced in the jungles of eastern Burma, referred to by the noble Lord, Lord Clarke, without access to adequate food, medicine, shelter or education. Many are on the run, in hiding in the jungles, pursued by the Burmese Army. Many others are held in relocation sites under the control of the Burmese Army, where their movement is restricted, access to food, medicine and education is limited or denied, and they are used for forced labour. In addition, more than 158,000 people are in refugee camps in Thailand; at least 200,000 Shan refugees in Thailand are without provision of camps; more than 50,000 are refugees in India; and thousands in Bangladesh, Malaysia and around the world.

The European Union passed an urgent resolution on 17 November calling on the UN Security Council to address the issue of Burma. The resolution also called for measures to strengthen the European Union common position on Burma, including "encouraging a blanket prohibition" on any new investments or loans to named Burmese state-owned enterprises by EU-registered companies. The resolution also recommended the creation of a European Union special envoy on Burma.

In addition to what has been said by previous speakers, Her Majesty's Government should be urged to take the following action. They should work to ensure that the issue of Burma is placed on the agenda of the UN Security Council as a matter of urgency; they should urge China and Russia not to exercise their vetos on a resolution at the UN Security Council; they should engage both China and India in discussions about Burma to highlight to them the trans-national problems of HIV/AIDS, human and drug trafficking, and urge them to support initiatives aimed at promoting political dialogue and national reconciliation.

Her Majesty's Government should urge the governments of the Kingdom of Thailand, India and Bangladesh to desist from forcibly repatriating those fleeing human rights violations in Burma; to desist from forcibly relocating Burmese refugees living in towns and cities into refugee camps; to provide greater access to refugee camps for non-governmental organisations; and to provide greater opportunities for education in the refugee camps, including access to
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC19
 
Internet facilities. They should urge the Government of the Kingdom of Thailand to provide recognition and shelter for displaced Shan people in Thailand.

I urge Her Majesty's Government to raise these issues and to support the action requested by the noble Lord, Lord Alton, in regard to the alleged genocide committed against the Karen, Karenni and Shan of Burma, and support the imposition of an arms and investment embargo on Burma.

28 Nov 2005 : Column GC21
 

The noble Lord, Lord Alton, has already mentioned some of the sources on which the EU can rely, and the noble Baroness, Lady Cox, and the noble Lord, Lord Chan, have added some more. The ILO would obviously be a source since its Application of Standards Committee had urged member states to review their direct foreign investment policies and report back to the governing body, which met earlier this month. It also considered the death threats against the ILO liaison officer and his predecessor, and the mass rallies and media campaign against the ILO which had seriously undermined the liaison officer's ability to perform his functions. The ILO asked the government of Myanmar to resume effective co-operation, and called on the regime to stop prosecuting those who were seeking redress on behalf of the victims of forced labour. It would be useful to know whether there has been any response from the SPDC, when the Minister replies.

In addition, there are the reports from the Secretary General's Special Rapporteur on Myanmar. Although he has not been allowed to enter the country since November 2003, Mr Pinheiro reports to the UN General Assembly, based on "a variety of independent and reliable sources". He singles out grave abuses against the ethnic communities for special mention. From the end of 2002 to October 2004, 157,000 people had been displaced by armed conflict, 240 villages destroyed or relocated, and between 700,000 and 1 million people have fled into Thailand and other nearby countries, reinforcing the figures already given by the noble Lord, Lord Alton. But, he added that sanctions were not the answer. Withdrawal of the Global Fund on AIDS, TB and malaria has hit the poor, but had no effect on the Government. He suggested instead that the international community should seek to establish a dialogue with the regime, though it is difficult to see how that could be achieved if the SPDC will not allow Mr Pinheiro into the country.

Mr Pinheiro suggested that states should be held accountable, presumably to the United Nations, and to the Security Council in particular, as the Motion suggests. The Secretary General, although noting in his latest report that 10 years have elapsed since he was first tasked by the General Assembly to use his good offices to facilitate democracy and reconciliation in Myanmar, and that the situation has actually worsened since General Khin Nyunt was ousted a year ago, merely addresses recommendations to the junta, which are certain to be ignored. When the national convention reconvenes next month, it will not contain representatives of the NLD or the Shan Nationalities League for Democracy. Restrictions on the freedom of movement of Daw Aung San Suu Kyi and other political leaders will not be lifted, and in fact the house arrests on her have just been renewed for another year. Party offices will not be allowed to reopen, nor will dissidents arrested, since the partial release of 249 prisoners in early July, be released.

Mr Annan warned that other aid groups would follow the Global Fund in withdrawing from Myanmar in the absence of moves towards reform, but
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC22
 
he held out the carrot of substantial aid in support of reconciliation and democracy if there are positive signs of change by the regime. How long has to elapse before conditions justifying the intervention of the Security Council lead to action under Chapter VII? In the impressive report referred to by the noble Lord, Lord Alton, Threat to the Peace, the authors examine six factors, some of which were present in each of the seven Chapter VII initiatives since 19990, but all of which are present in Myanmar. They could have added Liberia, Cote d'Ivoire, Somalia, Burundi and DRC, reinforcing still further the precedents for acting on Burma.

The arguments for doing nothing were summarised by the noble Lord, Lord Triesman, in the exchanges we had on 27 October, and we can all guess where the blockage comes from. China is a substantial investor in Myanmar's natural resources, and enjoys a virtual monopoly which could be challenged if the US and European companies entered the market. No doubt the army, which controls all inward investment, makes deals with the Chinese—Myanmar is one from the bottom in Transparency International's corruption index—and this is no doubt why the people are among the poorest in the world. The generals would be reluctant to give up power, and the Chinese would oppose moves under Chapter VII that might threaten the scam. The UK's general policy, as I understand it, is not to support resolutions unless they are sure that they are not going to be vetoed, and that they will get the nine votes needed for approval. But there have been exceptions, notably when the US, the UK and Spain tried to get a second resolution on Iraq in March 2003. Nobody pleaded then, as the noble Lord, Lord Triesman, did last week, that there was not a majority in the Security Council, or that the neighbours would not like it. So I suggest that the UK and any other member states of the Security Council who will join us, should issue a statement saying that they are minded to table a resolution to smoke out the objectors and launch a campaign to persuade them that Myanmar is, indeed, a threat to the peace.

In the view of the UK, as set out by the Foreign Secretary in his confidential memorandum of 8 March 2002 to the Prime Minister on the legality of action against Iraq, the use of force may be justified if the action is taken to prevent an overwhelming humanitarian catastrophe. It was under this rubric that force was used, and rightly so in my opinion, against the Serb oppressors in Kosovo. As the Foreign Secretary emphasised, the catastrophe has to be clear and well documented, there must be no other means short of the use of force which could prevent it, and the measures must be proportionate. The massive displacements, destruction of homes and livelihoods, mass rapes and killings in Myanmar undoubtedly satisfy the test, and we have a moral responsibility, at least, to make out the case and lay it before the Security Council.

Lord Howell of Guildford: Everyone is immensely grateful to the noble Lord, Lord Alton, for tabling this

Question and for his untiring efforts, to which many references have been made, in this and, indeed, in many other causes. How can we unlock the situation? There are two issues which I would like to try and set out and give shape to in an effort to carry that purpose forward.

First, a lesser but important dilemma is that most people agree that one wants to have as little to do as possible with this repulsive regime. Therefore, the instinct is naturally to break links, cut off communications and so on, and to discourage investment, which to some extent has been done—and there is an even stronger argument for the discouragement. But at the same time, we hear the word "dialogue" mentioned in the debate, and that keeping lines open and particular lines of communication and information is part of the strategy going forward towards a nation which finally throws off, with help, this hideous regime with which it has been saddled. I think that it was my colleague in a debate in another place on the same subject who mentioned the absence of television in Burma. We all know from our experience of the collapse of the Soviet empire and the hideous Communist regime, that it was the television and, later on, the mobile telephone, which provided the window to the outside world that led people to say that they simply would not go on tolerating this fantastic difference between their ugly circumstances and the better circumstances the outside world could offer.

I do not have an answer to the dilemma. But one has to be careful in following too vigorously the path of cutting off every link and remember that there are some kinds of links that we must preserve.
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC24
 

The second is the much bigger issue which has arisen in almost every speech that your Lordships have made this afternoon. That is, leaders of responsible nations are ready to say that they of course recognise these abuses and the tyranny, that they are totally against it, that crimes against humanity have almost certainly been committed, and that it is all very deplorable. Three of them are members of the Security Council—the United States, the United Kingdom and France. They have made it quite clear—I hope I am not doing the Minister's job—that they want this matter brought to the Security Council's attention. They have not denied that that is what they would like in certain circumstances and if there were not certain difficulties, which I will come to in a moment. In a sense, Burma is not just a miserable sink of atrocities in itself, but it is a fountain of poison which is infecting the entire world, so we are all involved from the start. There is no question that this is a very bad phenomenon. Whether one can go as far as the Havel report and say that it is a threat to the peace of the world is a debatable matter. In a sense it is. It is killing young people across the globe by its existence and policies. I think all this has been put with immense clarity by the UN Special Rapporteur, Professor Paulo Pinheiro. He has made the case as clearly as one could. Yet, here we are and we are stuck.

It appears to be the view of HMG, which we will hear in a moment, and others that currently they cannot get hold of this eight. They cannot command the eight. They can get nearly that, but not quite the eight members on top of the United States.

28 Nov 2005 : Column GC25
 
the sincerity and feeling behind such a suggestion—of bringing Myanmar onto the agenda now, in a sense, in a forcible way, would that simply result in a Russian and Chinese veto? If so, would that give just the kind of succour and help to the junta to carry on with its cruelty that it is looking and hoping for and not at the moment getting? That seems to me to be the dilemma. I do not begin to see the way through it. But, if that is the danger, then we must proceed with great care. Some advances have been made, maybe as a result of things said in your Lordships' House. The almost absurd folly of giving Myanmar the chairmanship of the ASEAN council has been averted. The ASEAN members must have realised that that would be too much and going too far and that has been headed off, so there is a small gain there. We would like to hear from the Government how, in carrying this forward, they will focus on the chief obstacles and overcome them—in particular, how they will focus on the attitude of China, to which the noble Lord, Lord Avebury, rightly referred. The danger is much bigger than Burma alone. The more that China is "harangued"—I choose my words carefully—in crude terms about the need to become democratic tomorrow morning, and so forth, which is the sort of speech I believe that the President of the United States made on his recent tour, the more China will follow its foreign policy. It will support all the rogue states to show that it is separate from the United States and can have a foreign policy of its own. That is the current posture of China. That is why it supports Burma, in particular.

However, China also supports many other rogue countries, some for very practical reasons relating to oil and resources. Burma is certainly included in that, as well as the Sudan and Venezuela. China has a massive investment and involvement in those areas. It supports other countries to further its policy—to put it in unparliamentary language—of "getting up the noses" of the Americans and the Bush administration. For example, China supports Mr Mugabe. There is no conceivable reason relating to raw materials to support him; it is entirely to irritate the Americans and the rest of us.

So that is the Chinese policy. Therefore, should we not examine those policies which appear to be driving China more and more into assisting and opening its arms to Burma and, in the other way, driving Burma and this hideous junta into the arms of China? The ugly fact is that the Chinese are becoming more involved in Burma. The junta is finding the delights of relying on the growing resources of Beijing extremely extractive, and the more it turns that way, the more it feels it is on a winning ticket. It may have reason for believing so because, unfortunately, we live in a world where the Chinese look out from Beijing and see that there has been a collapse in American soft power. A recent survey showed that in all Muslim countries and, I believe, 80 per cent of all western countries, opinion was in favour of stronger links with China and weaker
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC26
 
links with the United States. That is the extraordinary situation we have reached, where China, which is by no means a democracy and has its own extremely ugly abuses and practices, is able to parade on the world stage as being preferable in the eyes of many countries to the United States. That gives great comfort to the Burmese junta and will continue to do so; and it will mean that however strongly we fight and feel about the hideous processes there, we will find that China stands in the way.

In the end, it comes down to wanting to do all the things that your Lordships have suggested—because they are right—but also realising that until the Chinese embrace of Burma can somehow be turned into a squeeze, our efforts will not have the effect we want and we will find ourselves blocked at the Security Council. That is my analysis. If the Minister tells me I am wrong, I am ready to accept that.

But the focus and goal of our policy should be to ensure that somehow China and Russia take a different view and realise that they are destroying their own interests in the long term and should reverse their policies. This would enable us to bring the matter before the United Nations. At that point sanctions could be put in place and other pressures brought to bear, thus leading to the release of the marvellous Aung San Suu Kyi and, at last, Burma will be brought into the light. Until then, I am afraid that I am a bit of a pessimist on the whole issue.

Community Relations

5.20 pm

Baroness Falkner of Margravine rose to ask Her Majesty's Government what steps they are taking to address the segregation of communities, and to enhance better community relations.

The noble Baroness said: This is a Cinderella subject, and not one greeted with much enthusiasm outside the circles of the usual suspects, many of whom are here at short notice, so let me say from the outset that I am most grateful to Committee Members who are here.

This is not a new debate: we have been here before. In looking up published research on the segregation of communities, I came across arguments around this subject in its current form going back to the 1970s and earlier. What is surprising is the persistence of the topic—just as it seems to recede from the research agenda, events take place which bring it back to the forefront. So, some 50 years after non-white immigration started to change the face of British cities,
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC32
 
and several changes of government later, one gets the impression that while much has changed, not so much has in fact, changed.

Why does segregation matter? Many would argue in the case of voluntary segregation that choosing a lifestyle which might include very little interaction with anyone dissimilar to oneself is what choice is ultimately about. In the case of involuntary segregation, where people's practical choices are rather limited, there could still be an argument that segregation of itself is not such a social ill, and that corresponding other factors need to be addressed. In the debate today I hope to suggest that in an increasingly globalised world, and in a country which is on an upward trajectory in hosting new immigrant populations, it is of fundamental importance that we get integration right. I should also say that, which ever side of the debate you might be on, most of us would agree with Trevor Phillips and the CRE that there is no silver bullet, and that we probably need to concentrate on a mix of equality, participation and interaction, recognising that none of them alone will do it. But first, a word about immigrants, from one who has been an immigrant herself.

In the past, settled immigrants have come in numbers from the old and new Commonwealths. While those from the new Commonwealth have been racially different, they have shared historical ties with Britain and therefore share the language, legal traditions and political cultures, and to some extent, even religion. We are now facing a situation where, as the IPPR study Beyond Black and White shows, new immigrants—since 1990—are different. Although some of them may be white, if they come from the regions of the former Soviet Union or Latin America, they will be unlikely to share other characteristics. Once race is disregarded, an Albanian will have less in common with someone indigenous to East Anglia than an Indian will have with someone from Ipswich. Therefore, the measures that public policy needs to concentrate on will also need to change over time.

In this newly recast debate on multiculturalism, I want to concentrate primarily on the issue of segregation and why it matters. Studies have been wheeled out across the board recently. On the whole, the literature which has been produced since the 2001 population census confirms what we have known for some time—that spatial and educational segregation is still with us. The figures are disturbing in that in high segregation areas such as Oldham, Bradford, or Blackburn and, to a lesser extent, in Slough, parts of Manchester and Birmingham, the relationship between segregation in neighbourhoods—which comprise electoral wards—and secondary schools is evident. But it appears that segregation in schools is now overtaking that of spatial segregation for children who happen to be of Caribbean, Pakistani, Bangladeshi and Sikh heritage. In plain English, that means that successive generations are not coming together through education, but are being educated in silos. In cases such as these, while equality might theoretically exist,
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC33
 
participation and interaction will not take place during the critical years which determine outcomes and life chances.

In addition to geography and education, it appears in recent literature that a further phenomenon is also at play. This is highlighted in a Joseph Rowntree Foundation study in collaboration with the University of Essex which has just been published. Called Migration and social mobility: the life chances of Britain's minority ethnic communities, the study looks at data from a longitudinal study of two cohorts in 1971 and 1981, and analyses their social mobility based on data from the 2001 census. Data from the 2001 census has particular relevance as it covered the issue of religious identity for the first time.

The study confirms that patterns of intergenerational social mobility vary by ethnic group, and looks at the impact of the "ethnic penalty" in explaining such differences. It additionally looks at whether patterns of intergenerational mobility vary by religious affiliation, to see whether that factor affects outcomes.

In summary—and in summarising, I admit that I will do no justice to the work of the author—the study finds that there is evidence to show that there is indeed a greater openness of society in relation to ethnic difference. This is demonstrated by the change in social class of children from migrant families, which shows that some ethnic groups have done better than others. The Indian success story is well known, and the class privilege, where it existed, has been maintained across generations, even among ethnic minorities.

But, disturbingly, the study also shows that ethnicity matters differently across different groups, and that Caribbeans and Pakistanis are more disadvantaged due to racial inequality than other non-white groups. It goes on to show the impact of religion, and particularly the penalty of being a Muslim. So for Pakistani and Bangladeshi origin cohorts, not only did class origins not work for them, neither did educational qualification. In other words, the "ethnic penalty" was compounded by the "Islam penalty".

In a secular framework, one would expect faith to be a matter for the private sphere. This is not to say that there would be no duty on the state to ensure legal equality and to enforce human rights norms relating to the freedoms to practice one's faith. It is just that one would not expect to find, as the evidence now suggests, that discrimination of a new kind would emerge as respects one particular faith.

This brings me to the consideration of diversity and culture within the debate on segregation. I was hoping that I could make this speech without mentioning multi-culturalism, not least because of the presence of the noble Lord, Lord Parekh, and his knowledge of these issues. But I will venture to say that setting academic research aside, we know that something is going wrong. Or at least we know that something is not quite right when we see trends moving in the wrong direction. The CRE commissioned surveys in 2004 and 2005 on the themes of immigration and integration. Key findings on "friendship circles" showed that there
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC34
 
has been a reduction of social mixing among all groups. So in 2005 the number of non-white Britons whose friends are all, or mainly, white, went down from 47 per cent to 37 per cent. However, white Britons remain consistent in knowing mainly white people—the figure was 94 per cent in 2004 and 95 per cent in 2005.

We also know that it cannot be good for community relations when education is becoming more, not less, segregated. I turn here to faith schools. My concern with the Government's commitment to increasing funding for faith schools is their differing impact on different communities. So I understand the Government's duty to be even-handed across communities and to pledge to increase its funding for more Muslim schools, which I broadly welcome. However, where communities are segregated by gender as well as by other criteria, as Muslim schools are, then the segregation is made more acute. So where a Muslim girl goes to a Muslim secondary school and lives her life in segregated circumstances in all other areas, it is entirely possible that she will find herself married in her late teenage years without ever having encountered relationships with males outside her immediate family. What life skills will she have developed in relation to the other sex when she goes out to work, in terms of her own parenthood when she bears children or in accessing mixed public services?

The report of the Community Cohesion Review Team—or the Cantle report, as we usually call it—was one of the best reviews of how communities see their problems. It detailed how some of the aspects of educational segregation could be ameliorated. Among these were the recommendations that schools should be twinned with others to compensate for lack of contact with other cultures in the school. The Minister's colleague in the other place, Margaret Hodge, the Member for Barking, has also expressed ideas along the lines of ensuring that attendance by a certain number of children outside the denomination should become a requirement for faith schools. Can the Minister tell us to what extent these recommendations are informing the Government's policy?

We also know that something is not working because of the Birmingham-style disturbances of a few months ago. They had echoes of the severe public order disturbances of Oldham and Burnley, which led to the Cantle review of 2001. Prior to that, we had the report of the noble Lord, Lord Ouseley, on the Bradford disturbances. All of those expert groups have seen segregation and alienation between communities as a factor. Although we used to interpret community cohesion as something socio-economic, there is now a consensus that it is broader and must consider things in the round, so education, employment poverty, social inequality and cultural diversity all play a part. Public policy must therefore be more than a joined-up approach to solving a problem here and now, it must be an oncoming processes.

In considering disadvantaged and disaffected communities, one recognises that people's identities are multifaceted and so are needs. That applies as
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC35
 
much to white as to non-white people, so an approach that seems sometimes to problematise groups as particularly deserving must become colour-blind and recognise that disadvantage is occurring to white as well as non-white people. The imperative is on us to share values, challenges and opportunities, but that cannot happen without a sense of trust in common institutions that are to serve the interests of all sections of society.

Much attention has been focused on community leaders and there is little doubt that they, the people on the ground, have done good work in defusing potential crises. However, there is also a valid question about community voice. Where communities are most isolated in terms of interaction with others, the fact of voice—whether it takes the form of visible representation in electoral office, which is still marginal, or whether it comes through the public service providers being somewhat representative of the communities they serve—becomes important. If we consider London, for example, the Met is conscious of the need to build trust among Muslims. Hence, recruitment programmes are aimed at them. Have other police forces and key agencies such as local authorities, health authorities and regeneration agencies taken on board the need to recruit and promote among the communities they serve?

In conclusion, I reiterate the need for shared values. Although the Government are making fledgling steps towards identifying what factors people might consider to occupy the common ground, we are far from reaching consensus. We need a dialogue within and between the mainstream and minority communities in an attempt to define what being a Briton means. From these Benches, we would welcome moves towards such a dialogue.

Lord Parekh: I begin by thanking the noble Baroness, Lady Falkner of Margravine, not only for raising the subject, but for speaking about it with such eloquence and force. I should like, first, to say something about the rather more boring academic part of this—the patterns of segregation—and, secondly, to say something about where we should be getting in encouraging a common sense of belonging. As the noble Baroness rightly said, there are parts of our country where there is considerable segregation leading, in many cases to parallel lives with limited contact among the people involved. Patterns of social contact and patterns of friendship are also severely limited. There are also intra-ethnic minority tensions, as we saw in the case of Birmingham.

That is one side of the picture. The other side is a little more encouraging. Dr Ludi Simpson of the University of Manchester, for example, in response to the remark of Mr Trevor Phillips, the chairman of the CRE, that we are somnambulating into segregated lives, has shown that that is not right overall. As he argues, the number of mixed neighbourhoods, for example, has increased from 864 in 1991 to 1,070 in 2001 and is likely to rise to 1,300 in 2011. In some cases
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC36
 
where segregation exists—Leicester, for example, or parts of London—it is either voluntary or a largely middle-class phenomenon. Therefore, although people may live separately, their lives are not separate because they tend to interact at workplaces, social situations and in various clubs to which they may belong.

It may also be useful to bear in mind that in this country, we have the highest rate or incidence of inter-ethnic marriage in Europe or the United States. Of Asian men, 19 per cent have white partners and 8 per cent of Asian women; 40 per cent of Afro-Caribbean men and 30 per cent of Afro-Caribbean women have white partners; 20 per cent of Chinese women have white partners. It is also striking that mixed-race children rather surprisingly constitute the third largest minority group in Britain, after Indians and Pakistanis.

When we talk about segregation, we ought to think not only of the economic and social terrain, we should also think about the civic and political level. I heard the following figures this morning at an important conference. They really pleased and surprised me. If we consider the voluntary work that the various groups do, the highest rate of voluntary work is among mixed-race people; the second highest is among Afro-Caribbeans; the third highest is among whites; and the fourth is Asians. If we consider political integration in terms of electoral registration and participation, the figures are again quite high. All that tends to show that no ethnic minority is a permanent preserve of a particular political party and that, although people may live separately in certain areas, they continue to interact as fellow citizens in the political and civic realm.

Although the figures are more ambiguous in their interpretation, I was also heartened by the BBC multi-culturalism poll produced on 10 August, one month after the horrible events of 7 July. The data were collected only two days earlier on 8 and 9 August. They show a country that is relatively at ease with itself in both minority and majority communities, but also not entirely sure where it is going. The following two sets of figures will indicate what I have in mind. When people were asked, "Do you think that multi-culturalism has made Britain a better place to live?" 62 per cent of white Britons said yes; 32 per cent said no. They were asked a second question: "Given the threat of terrorism, do you think that our society should be more concerned with the rights of minorities or with national security?" In response, 58 per cent said that we should be more concerned with the rights of minorities, because that is ultimately what we stand for and 30 per cent said no, we should be less concerned with them.

I now consider the same poll as it relates to Muslims. They were asked similar questions. They were asked, "Do you think that it is vital that immigrants who come here should learn English?"; 90 per cent said yes. They were asked: "Do you think that they should adapt some of the values of British society, especially in relation to gender equality?"; 95 per cent said that it was vital that they should accept the rights of women
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC37
 
as equal citizens. When asked, "What about accepting loyalty to the institutions of our society?", 91 per cent said that all immigrants must accept the authority of British institutions. There was some unease about other values, such as free speech and the trade-off between free speech and freedom of religion, where the figures were not as high or as exciting. However, if you were to ask that question to philosophers such as me, you would not get a very different answer, because I am not sure that the American doctrine of fetishising free speech is the best way to organise a sensible society.

That is a rather mixed picture, but one with much promise and good sense. If we want to build good relations, we should cultivate and foster a common sense of belonging among our people. I emphasise that phrase: common sense of belonging, because it is quite different from talking about Britishness—a term that I find offensive and philosophically incoherent. I want to explain why. I want a society at ease with itself whose members feel that they belong to each other, form part of a single community and know that they can count on each other to look after and stand up for them in times of need. That is what ultimately matters. Does that require that we share exactly the same values or exactly the same ethno-cultural characteristics? That is where the idea of Britishness becomes deeply problematic.

I have observed and spoken in many countries in recent years about that sense of national identity and common belonging. I can say with some confidence, as an academic, that the term Britishness is peculiar to us. I have not heard the term Frenchness, Indianness or Americanness. How we got that term, I am not entirely sure. The difficulty with that concept is as follows. It implies that what binds us together is a set of shared characteristics that each of us carries in our heads or our bodies. To belong to a country is a relationship; it is not a set of empirical characteristics. If I were to ask someone of what Britishness consisted, either they would talk in terms of certain values, such as kindness, tolerance, and so on, to which the immediate answer is, "Are you suggesting that those values are peculiar to us and that the French, Germans and others do not have them?" If values are moral values, by definition, they are universal and cannot be specific to a particular country. Norms, decency and other things may be peculiar, but values may not.

The other thing that may be peculiar is certain ethno-cultural or psychological characteristics: a stiff upper lip; a love of tea; a love of animals or gardens; and so on. But I do not think that anyone wants to suggest that we cannot belong together unless we all love animals and have the same characteristics. When one talks about values and psychological characteristics, there is a danger that you can stuff the concept of Britishness with whatever happens to suit you ideologically. Therefore, the definition becomes a lever, an instrument, with which to mould a people in a particular image. So, rather than talk about Britishness, we should be talking about how we can
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC38
 
foster a common sense of belonging among our people, such that we know ourselves as members of a single community.

Peter Kellner of you.gov this morning used an interesting phrase that I liked. He asked whether we will think of national identity as a furnished apartment—here it is, if you want to come to occupy it, these are the terms? Or will we think of it as a shared freehold that we are all collectively involved in shaping? I should like to think of being British and all British national identity as a shared freehold that we are all involved in shaping in a direction in which we can see a reflection of ourselves and that we can collectively own. What are the conditions under which such a common belonging can be fostered? I should have thought that they included such basic things as equal respect, absence of discrimination, broadly equal life chances and equal confidence that our major institutions are impartial and look after us all.

In other words, if we talk of Britishness, we talk of moulding people in a certain way; if we talk of common belonging, we immediately think of policies and institutions that we need to develop. We should be concentrating on policies and institutions that we need. When we do that, we begin to see that discrimination, although it has declined, remains fairly high; that there is still considerable resentment of ethnic minorities in certain circles; and, as the noble Baroness, Lady Falkner, pointed out, that the economic life chances of some groups of people are unequal.

Let us consider unemployment figures. For white Britons, there is 5 per cent unemployment, compared to 12 per cent among Pakistanis and 16 per cent among Bangladeshis. If we consider the percentage of people whose income falls below half the national average, it is 28 per cent among white Britons but 60 per cent among Pakistanis and Bangladeshis. Even when both parents work, the percentage of people who depend on means-tested support, it is 8 per cent among white Britons but 40 per cent among Pakistanis and Bangladeshis. I could have given similar sets of figures about education or other areas of life.

I conclude by saying that, by and large, our pluralistic model of integration has worked reasonably well. We still have some way to go, but we are headed in the right direction. In that context, it may be useful to contrast that with the republican model of integration in France. There is nothing wrong with that model. That is another way in which a society can move and different societies have different cultures and traditions, but what we see in France is striking. The riots that took place had nothing to do with material deprivation or poverty, because the benefit provisions in France are just as good as ours and, in many respects, better than in the United States. Despite that, the riots took place. The question is why. The answer is threefold. First, lots of those involved felt excluded from the French society—they felt, "We belong to this society but this society marginalises us by confining us to certain estates and practises heavy discrimination".
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC39
 

For example, a French-sounding person making 100 applications would expect to be interviewed 75 times. Someone with an Arabic-sounding name would expect to be interviewed only 14 out of 100 times. The other problem in France is police harassment. People are constantly picked up and resent it. The third thing that was important was a feeling that they were being constantly culturally attacked. The hijab was banned, Arabic languages were not allowed in school, and so on. In short, when a community feels thoroughly excluded, marginalised and left out—money is thrown at it so that it does not starve, but no society, no group of people, lives by bread alone—that is when things begin to go wrong. I hope that we have at least guarded against some of those dangers, but unless we continue to push in the same direction with greater vigour we may stir up trouble—not exactly of the kind that we saw in France, but a society that has so far done so well should not relax, just in case our pluralistic model of integration runs into difficulty in more or less the same way as the republican model.

Lord Chan: I also thank the noble Baroness, Lady Falkner of Margravine, for securing this short debate and for her forceful speech. This is an issue of growing concern for all who want to promote better community relations in Britain. I find myself in the invidious position of speaking not only after the noble Baroness, but after the noble Lord, Lord Parekh, who is an academic of international reputation in the field of communities and people from different backgrounds who settle in countries to which they have migrated. So I will speak from my experience of Merseyside and from my participation in voluntary organisations and government agencies, including being a commissioner of the Commission for Racial Equality in the 1990s.

Segregation is not a new phenomenon: it invariably led to conflict between the separated communities in Northern Ireland in the last three decades of the 20th century. In mainland Britain, segregation has occurred among people divided by race, ethnic group or religion. The noble Lord, Lord Parekh, gave recent research findings showing positive signs that integration is taking place and that, although there may be much alarming news, there is also lots of good news.

Going back to 1981, rioting by inner-city residents including those in London, Bristol, Birmingham, Liverpool, Manchester and Leeds, involved the poor and unemployed who lived in sub-standard housing. In Liverpool's Toxteth district, half the population was from black and other minority ethnic groups whose young people left school without any hope of gainful employment. When they applied for social security benefits, they were humiliated and frustrated by petty bureaucracy and local government offices. In addition to the deprivation that they experienced in housing, community facilities and employment, the residents of Toxteth had increasingly unpleasant
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC40
 
experiences with the police, who prevented them from moving out of their district. Frustration generated by those depressing circumstances and police stop-and-search encounters led to an explosion of anger and destruction.

We have heard of similar events in France but, as the noble Lord, Lord Parekh, said, the circumstances there were not socio-economic. However, there were elements of being marginalised because of one's race and religion that are important.

Twenty years later, in 2001, four towns in Lancashire and Yorkshire with segregation of mainly Pakistani Asian communities from their white neighbours had occurred in housing and education, similar violence erupted with young men burning cars and fighting police officers.

The noble Baroness, Lady Falkner, has given us some details about how we can analyse these issues.

Major factors in the segregation of communities are housing and education in exclusive racial settings—and it is certainly worse where there is also gender segregation—an absence of gainful employment and a lack of contact with other communities. Among disadvantaged ethnic groups, housing is usually substandard with overcrowding, unemployment is widespread, educational attainments of young people are very low, health is poor and people feel isolated from the mainstream of society.

The fact that the four suicide bombers of the London Underground and bus service on 7 July this year were from the poor districts of Leeds has led to generalisations. We have heard from the chairman of the Commission for Racial Equality that Britain is "sleepwalking to segregation". Linked to this concept of segregation is that of the failure of multiculturalism in Britain, which he defined as promoting adherence to minority cultures and isolation from and antagonism towards mainstream values of what it means to be a British citizen. However, I am delighted that the noble Lord, Lord Parekh, has explained how that is not true.

The question is whether the chairman of the CRE's analysis of segregation is accurate? Is it possible to extrapolate from the small number of segregated ethnic minority groups to the general conclusion that maintaining a multiplicity of cultures in Britain leads to segregation and works against integration with mainstream society?

I am not convinced that that interpretation of the facts is accurate and true. My views would be closer to those of my noble friend Lord Ouseley, a former chairman of the CRE. In an article in the House magazine of 14 November, he concludes that,

    "prejudice is prevalent in all communities; many immigrants are compelled to settle in deprived areas that can be difficult to escape; and social and racial integration cannot happen on unequal terms".

It is clear that the vast majority of black and ethnic minority groups living in Britain are not segregated from mainstream society, although some, particularly the older people who are not fluent in the English language, may be isolated. But even these older people receive access to services through bilingual staff,
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC41
 
mostly employed through local government or neighbourhood renewal grants. Their needs are catered for by mainstream services.

We know that every child born in Britain is educated in the English language without parents having to pay fees, unless they choose to do so. Educational attainment is high among Chinese and Indian children, according to Ofsted, but low in Pakistani and Bangladeshi children. As a result, the latter have difficulty obtaining gainful employment outside of food catering and low-skilled jobs. But advice and training are available to all who seek to improve themselves. That occurs in these particular groups of people. There is certainly a need for more mentors to help young people from some communities and to encourage them to achieve higher educational levels. I am of course particularly concerned about what the noble Baroness said about faith schools with gender segregation that reflects similar gender segregation within their home and community.

Housing renewal projects, whether by local authority or housing associations, allocate accommodation predominantly to a mix of ethnic groups, with the majority being allocated to white families. That is reflective of the community people live in. This gives people from different ethnic and cultural backgrounds exposure to each other and should promote co-existence.

That has certainly been the experience of Vietnamese refugees who arrived on Merseyside in 1980 and have integrated with existing local residents.

Of course, people of any ethnic group tend to maintain friends from the same background who share a common culture, language and experience. To that we can also add social class and status. But this should not restrict them from getting to know their neighbours, whether they be white or not. Sadly, we know that our view of community cohesion is slow to establish itself. Multiculturalism that confines people to their own race and cultural group is a narrow minority view. Such a view of community is clearly not sustainable in Britain. Even in countries where different ethnic groups live in larger proportions than they do here, such as in Malaysia, an accommodation has developed over decades to enable all to progress according to their skills and to live in peace and harmony.

One dominant feature of the current situation here is the role of extremist groups using Islam as a basis or excuse for segregation, separation and terrorism against mainstream society. Legislation exists to deal with this form of extremism, but more needs to be done to support communities to live with the values of a civil society that they can accept. There has been a breakdown in community life and an increase in violence that is most noticeable in urban settings, and is associated with a lack of respect for other people and their property. Life in housing estates is becoming lawless and antisocial. Politicians, including the Prime Minister, have identified this depressing trend.

I close my remarks by mentioning a refreshing contribution to the restoration of community values that has come from the new Archbishop of York, Dr
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC42
 
John Sentamu. He has appealed to white Britons to rediscover the Christian basis of their personal and community values. If his advice is taken seriously by the Churches, we could once again see peace and co-operation in inner-city communities where today family breakdown, drug taking and crime have distorted people's values and blighted communities.

I look forward to the Minister's response to the Question posed by the noble Baroness, Lady Falkner.

Baroness Ludford: I add my thanks to my noble friend Lady Falkner of Margravine for instigating this useful debate. I do not know how many noble Lords have been to 19 Princelet Street in Spitalfields, where a diverse team of unpaid enthusiasts is trying to create Europe's first museum of immigration. It is an inspiring antidote to discouragement about the challenge of diversity. Built in 1719 by one rather English-sounding Samuel Worrall, it later became the home of a Huguenot silk weaving family who had escaped persecution in France. The family prospered, became rich and moved on. The elegant house was subdivided and tenanted by a variety of craftsmen. The Irish moved through the district, to be followed by Jewish immigrants. In 1869 a synagogue was erected at the back of the house, and 100 years later it became empty and derelict. Meanwhile, the Spitalfields Brick Lane area became predominantly Bangladeshi and Muslim. Eventually the Spitalfields Centre was formed to acquire 19 Princelet Street. Emergency repairs were made and the team now opens the house when it can to display the "Suitcases and Sanctuary" exhibition, visited by local schoolchildren.

It is something of a reproach to London that while New York has Ellis Island and the Tenement Museum, and both Toronto and Melbourne have museums of immigration, we cannot manage to fund a similar celebration of the richness and economic contribution that newcomers over the centuries have brought. So far as I know, there is no museum of immigration in Europe. So step forward generous donors, whether public or private, and help to establish the first European museum of immigration here in London. The only interest I need to declare is that I am a Friend of 19 Princelet Street and a Member of the European Parliament for London.

Recalling the history of Spitalfields is a useful reminder that there is nothing wrong with concentrations of people in a particular community. If you think of the Irish community historically in north London, of Caribbeans in Brixton, of Chinatown, of Indians and spices in Southall, of Cypriots in Green Lanes, of the Jewish community and kosher food in Golders Green, of the Korean community in Kingston, it is quite natural for people to congregate where their language, culture and food first gained a toehold, and then businesses and shops that serve their needs open. It is a great asset to a city to have particular "quartiers" or villages where you know you will find a critical mass of goods and cuisine of a
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC43
 
particular kind. To look at things from a reverse perspective, as it were, just think of the Brits flocking together in the Dordogne or the Costa del Sol.

The problem comes when concentration turns into segregation; when clusters are closed and inward-looking rather than open and permeable. Let us be even-handed: there are problems of exclusion and a refusal to integrate from the gated communities of the rich as well as the modest terraces or tower blocks of the poor.

I read in this week's New Statesman magazine about the discouraging situation in Northern Ireland. The author labelled the situation as "apartheid", which is a strong term. Eighty per cent of people apparently will not use the nearest shops or swimming pool because they are located on the other side of the Catholic-Protestant divide. Nine out of 10 children attend segregated schools, and there are separate bus stops either side of a gate in the so-called peace line—that is, a high brick wall—which divides a Catholic from a Protestant community. This is so that no one has to stand waiting among people of a different religion. Think about that. Imagine if it was a gender or a racial divide—or, indeed, a religious one—in England, Scotland or Wales. How on earth can such behaviour be tolerated within the United Kingdom?

The challenge we face is to give people enough respect and room to feel confident in their distinctive racial, religious, cultural or other identity while encouraging them to share it with the rest of us and contribute to forging a common bond of civic identity as Britons and Europeans, guaranteed the same human rights and obeying the same laws. I am not among those people who have rejected multiculturalism, even if such rejection seems to be the fashion. I am convinced that we all—white people included—have multi or multiple identities and interests.

But to me "multicultural" has never meant a fragmentation of society into myriad unconnected pieces. It is certainly a jigsaw, but one which can and must be assembled into a coherent whole. We are seeking integration not assimilation, which is where, in my opinion, the French go wrong. I have been involved in efforts in the European Parliament to suggest that the banning of the hijab is not terribly helpful in France or continental countries.

In ensuring the respect and self-confidence I spoke of, there is a very important role for laws and policies to combat discrimination and social and economic inequality. Although EU anti-discrimination law from 2000 did not oblige major changes in our own race relations Acts, it was for some member states—and I include the 10 which came in last year—a new and major step forward. Some member states are overdue in their implementation and are being taken to the European Court of Justice for infringement of these EU laws. At a time when there are worrying developments in some member states on attitudes to equality—most notably in Poland—it is crucial that a strong signal is sent by the whole EU that anti-discrimination provisions are among the most
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC44
 
important of EU values. I strongly believe that the United Kingdom has a major part to contribute to that exercise.

The EU package obliged for the first time that religious discrimination at work should be outlawed. It is important that the ability to track progress in eradicating discrimination in employment should be bolstered by the keeping of statistics. Unfortunately, in many countries of the European Union that is a very hot potato. There is a great deal of debate going on, but if you use the term "ethnic monitoring" in the European Parliament the balloon goes up. Among the French, Germans and Swedish—for different reasons—the idea that you should keep data on the ethnic or religious identity of people is strongly resisted.

Another initiative put forward at the EU level is the introduction of an EU law banning race hate crime. The proposal would also criminalise incitement to religious hatred but only if it was a pretext for expressions of racial hatred. It is similar to the suggestions made in this House in relation to our domestic law. As the British Government proposed the formulation of the European law, it is rather surprising that they were not consistent on the UK front and took a different route for domestic law.

It is a great shame that EU governments and the Council of Ministers have been unable to reach agreement on the law as formulated. I understand there were difficulties over condoning or trivialising historical events, especially concerning the Holocaust—perhaps the mirror image, one could say, of problems over the glorification of terrorist acts. I regret that an effort for compromise could not have been made to provide a common definition of racist crimes in Europe. It is a gap in the catalogue of European law.

Establishing a solid legal framework of non-discrimination is essential to the achievement of integration. It demonstrates clearly that integration is a two-way street, not a one-way effort. I firmly believe that more could be done to inform local communities about the language, culture and origins of the immigrant and ethnic minority groups living locally to them, even if some of those pen sketches might be a little simplistic. Some knowledge is surely better than none. Sometimes we are a little too precious and assume that people know things that they do not.

The recent report of the European Union's racism monitoring centre highlighted the schizophrenic attitudes widely held in Europe—towards immigrant workers in particular—where some are happy for them to fill gaps in the labour market, especially in the jobs that the host society is not keen to do, but also demand strict controls on immigration and are not sufficiently alert to race discrimination. There is a need for much greater clarity, including in the rights and obligations of all parties. Of course, an obligation not to discriminate on grounds of gender or sexual orientation is as important as the eradication of racial and religious inequality. The EU should also impress
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC45
 
on partner countries such as Pakistan that we expect religious minorities to be protected from prosecution and attack.

EU Ministers are addressing the question of how to stop radicalisation and extremism, especially among Muslims. They refer to a problem of perception in the policies of European and other western countries, but as long as we are prepared to practise, tolerate or be complicit in abduction, illegal imprisonment without charge, rendition and torture, the problem is more than one of perception. The EU has to be a lot more determined and vigorous in practising the highest standards of human rights, not hand a propaganda victory to terrorists.

The European Union has been working for several years on how best to develop policies for integration, which is a useful exercise at EU level to share knowledge and best practice. It produced a Green Paper in September containing various proposals. No doubt the Minister will know all about that. In my view, it is essential that we should see this as a dynamic process. There is not one static society into which newcomers integrate—the "furnished apartment", as the noble Lord, Lord Parekh described it—and we should all change as we adjust to diversity, the "shared freehold", while not lowering our standards of law, democracy and equality. I welcome the proposal for a European integration fund of about £1 billion—1.7 billion euros—over the seven year period 2007–13, the next budgetary period, which has yet to be agreed. This has very much been championed by the European Parliament.

I have considerable reservations about how suggestions for temporary work permits, whether put forward by our own or other European Governments, or by the UN Global Commission on International Migration, can advance the cause of integration. It can undermine the motivation to commit and participate if the intention from the beginning is that a person will not stay. Life often does not work out in accordance with bureaucrats' intentions, as experience of Turkish "Gastarbeiter" in Germany—still there after 30 or 40 years—has shown.

Facility in the local language is crucial and more effort needs to be made throughout Europe on the issue. The provision of free language lessons is a good investment. There was much comment, even mirth, about the citizenship test here recently. I will not dwell on it, and I did not have the courage to test myself. I am not convinced that a test is the best way forward but we do need to spruce up our citizenship credentials all round, including knowledge of how the EU works.

Lastly, I believe that although we do have problems, the opinion polls show, as the noble Lord, Lord Parekh, suggests, a lot of promise and a lot of good sense. I conclude by saying that I do not have entirely the same reservations about the term "Britishness" as the noble Lord, Lord Parekh. To me, Britishness is a sense of unity in diversity—of mutual respect and belonging, not uniformity. I think that we can convey that understanding to the rest of Europe.

28 Nov 2005 : Column GC47
 
speech. As to the segregation of communities—or, possibly, put round the other way, failure to integrate—obviously there are more problems with some communities than with others.
