6.20 pm

Lord Bassam of Brighton: Along with all other noble Lords who have spoken in the debate, I too am grateful to the noble Baroness, Lady Falkner of Margravine, for initiating it. In her remarks the noble Baroness said that this is something of a minority interest. It is a shame if we view it in that way. This is an issue we must all address and collectively we should be interested in it because it is important. As my noble friend Lord Parekh said and the noble Lord, Lord Chan, made clear, this has been a matter for public debate over many decades and is not something from which we can turn away; we need actively to engage in it.

I am also grateful to the noble Baroness for providing me with an opportunity to set out some of the steps the Government are taking to address the apparent segregation of some communities and better enhance community relations generally. Comments by the chair of the Commission for Racial Equality, Mr Trevor Phillips, suggesting that we are "sleepwalking to segregation" have raised the profile of the debate on segregation. I share with my noble friend Lord Parekh an interest in the academic studies undertaken in this field. It is worth noting that prominent academics on segregation in the UK such as Ceri Peach, Danny Dorling and Ludi Simpson have presented evidence that ethnic segregation is, at most, stable for some minority ethnic groups and declining for others. It is also worth noting that according to our own 2005 Home Office Citizens' Survey, which is a nationally representative and more robust measure, some 80 per cent of people definitely agreed or tended to agree that their local area was a place where people from different backgrounds got on well together. That figure has not changed by much over time.

Nevertheless, the Government are aware that segregation is an issue that it would only be prudent to guard against. We have put in place a series of programmes in the housing, education and employment sectors to prevent this. It is important to establish at the outset that we have a common understanding of what we mean by segregation. I feel that it is best described as the tendency for demographic groups to be concentrated in their distribution across neighbourhoods, schools and occupations. It is also important to note that the issue is complex with potentially many subtle social, economic and geographic factors at play. I agree with the noble Baroness, Lady Ludford, that segregation
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC49
 
can have positive as well as negative impacts on social processes. The concentration of minority groups through residential segregation can allow groups to maintain a certain social cohesion. We should welcome and celebrate that in certain circumstances because they develop shared values and social networks.

The debate on segregation is complicated by the limitations of available research, including a lack of focus on the effects of migration. Other factors include the conflation of population growth and population distribution; changes over time in ethnicity classification and ward boundaries; a lack of consensus about the suitability of different measures of concentration; and the fact that residential concentration provides only a limited explanation for any lack of integration. Research in the UK is fairly limited and based largely on a comparison of 1991 and 2001 census data, which consider changing patterns in the location of different racial groups. Recently claims have been made that ethnic segregation in the UK is increasing between certain ethnic groups, yet research also exists which counters that assertion. As a result, it is safe to say that there is no real consensus of opinion on how ethnic segregation in the UK has changed over time.

Analysis by Dorling and Rees based on the 2001 census demonstrates that white polarisation increased from 3 per cent in 1991 to 4 per cent in 2001. In contrast, the degree of spatial polarisation of all ethnic minority groups has either fallen or stayed the same for Bangladeshis and other Asian groups. More recently published research using the 1991 and 2001 census by Professor Ludi Simpson at Manchester University, mentioned by my noble friend Lord Parekh, provides evidence that residential segregation is not increasing in England and Wales. That research, based on 8,850 electoral wards, including major cities in England and Wales, suggests that the migration patterns of white and other ethnic groups are very similar and are influenced by factors such as social mobility. This undermines the argument about the "white flight" from inner cities. Simpson also stressed that the increase in the relatively younger minority ethnic population results from demography; that is, differences in birth rates rather than migration. In short, the available data do not provide strong evidence of "white flight" occurring on a large scale, although there is some evidence of increased segregation—specifically, white people moving out of inner city areas—largely seen by academics as consistent with the broader trend of counter-urbanisation and an aging population.

In Britain, the prominent social geographers I have referred to, but also including Deborah Phillips, Ron Johnson and Tony Champion, have independently concluded that there are no ghettos in British cities based on 1991 census data. The "ghettos" reference is made to the extent to which, first, a single ethnic or racial group forms the whole population of the residential district; and, secondly, most members of that ethnic group are found in such areas.
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC50
 

Academic Michael Poulsen, from Macquarie University in Australia, recently presented a paper based on the two most recent censuses, quoting material for 16 cities in the UK, which suggested that despite most ethnic groups mixing with the white population, some Asian enclaves existed which were growing in both size and isolation. Comparisons between the 1991 and 2001 censuses by other academics, such as Ceri Peach at Oxford University, suggest that across the country as a whole segregation is actually declining as people from minority ethnic backgrounds become wealthier and move out of inner city areas. Although there are individual wards where the minority population, particularly those from Bangladeshi and Pakistani backgrounds, is especially concentrated, these are the exception. According to Peach, for the Afro-Caribbean population in London segregation has actually decreased in every census since 1961.

Professor Simpson's study last year examined Bradford's population and concluded that segregation did not increase in the decade between 1991 and 2001. However, Simpson recognises that segregation in Bradford is high and points out that this is unchanged. As the south Asian population has grown, inevitably it has taken not only some housing previously occupied by white groups in areas where the south Asian population has traditionally settled, but also some in new areas which have become mixed as a consequence.

For all ethnic minority groups identified by the census, the indices of segregation fell between 1991 and 2001, according to Dorling in an article in the Observer of 25 September this year. He claims that these indices actually fell fastest for black and "other Asian" people. Furthermore, although in absolute numerical terms increases in minority ethnic populations have been greatest in areas where minorities were already concentrated, this is consistent with a pattern of natural growth and continued immigration to join established family members. Lupton and Power in their study concluded that the white population is becoming less isolated from minority ethnic groups as these groups spread and grow in a larger number of areas. But, at the same time, in certain inner urban neighbourhoods minority ethnic groups are becoming more isolated as their numbers grow and white populations dwindle.

Research from the University of Bristol indicates that some schools are more segregated than their neighbourhoods. However, it is worth noting that academics such as Danny Dorling at the University of Leeds argue that this has been measured at one point in time, hence it does not represent a trend towards spatial racial segregation.

According to recent research conducted by the CRE/YouGov, for 95 per cent of white Britons, most or all of their friends are also white. One needs to set against that the Home Office citizenship survey which indicated that in 2003 a third of all white respondents—that is 33 per cent—claimed that they had friends from non-white ethnic groups. Two-thirds of Asian and Chinese and over four-fifths of black
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC51
 
people claimed they had friends from different ethnic groups to their own ethnicity. According to that survey, people were most likely to regularly meet and talk with people of different ethnic origin to themselves at local shops (45 per cent), in the work environment (40 per cent) and at restaurants, pubs, cinemas and community centres (38 per cent). Twenty per cent of the white group claimed that they did not regularly meet or speak with people of a different ethnic origin to themselves compared to 5 per cent of the Asian group and 5 per cent of the Chinese group. Only 2 per cent of the black group did not regularly meet or speak with people of a different origin to themselves, the lowest out of all ethnic groups.

Almost two-thirds of Asian and black people regularly meet and speak with people of different ethnic origin to themselves at local shops. In addition, 36 per cent of the black group claimed that they regularly met and talked with people of different ethnic origin to themselves at places of worship—five times greater than the percentage of white people. Interestingly, although 23 per cent of white people regularly met or spoke with people of a different ethnic origin to them in their neighbourhood, the proportion of minority ethnic people to meet or speak with people of a different ethnic origin to them in their neighbourhood was twice as high.

These data suggest that a large percentage of people from different ethnic groups have the opportunity to meet and speak with each other through a range of channels. However, meeting people from different ethnic groups in the neighbourhood and at local shops was relatively more common for minority ethnic people than for white people. That underlines the importance of localities bringing together people from different backgrounds. In summary, there is evidence of ethnic minority concentration which varies regionally across the UK. However, the extent to which this is segregation is open to debate. Data from the 2003 citizenship survey, to which I referred earlier, support the view that minority ethnic and white people are not ethnically segregated.

Let me turn to some of the preventive steps the Government are taking with regard to forms of segregation and self-segregation. On housing, one of the practical measures we have taken to prevent communities becoming more segregated is our choice-based lettings scheme, which we believe takes a more sophisticated approach to tackling perceptions that some areas are "white" or "black" than setting quotas. This gives people a choice of social housing but, crucially, in many cases has provided people with practical support in moving into areas which they may not traditionally have seen as being for them. Housing management can contribute much to reducing segregation and fostering community cohesion through the delivery of core housing management services, including the provision of new housing opportunities through allocation and lettings,
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column GC52
 
community participation, involvement and development, tenancy support and management and a better developed and more culturally sensitive and appropriate service delivery mechanism.

Education is a sphere in which it is extremely important to achieve well-balanced intakes. We endorse the use of banding in the schools White Paper as it tends to produce genuinely comprehensive school populations. Building on the good work which already takes place in many areas, the White Paper also sets out our plans to further develop networking and collaboration among schools, such as between schools with very different ethnic populations and religious influences.

The schools White Paper also aims to ensure that choice is more widely available to all, not just for those who can pay for it. This will be supported by extending rights to free transport for children from poorer families to go to the three nearest secondary schools within six miles of their home. That will extend the choice of school for the poorest in our society. We also aim to introduce better information for all parents when their children enter primary and secondary school. Dedicated choice advisers will help the least well-off parents to exercise their discretion. Local authorities will have a new duty to promote choice and diversity in access to school places and transport.

There are a number of other initiatives, not associated with admission policies, which contribute to greater social cohesion. These include citizenship education in secondary schools as part of the national curriculum to teach pupils about the diversity of national, regional, religious and ethnic identities in the UK, and the need for mutual respect, tolerance and understanding. It provides a context within which schools may discuss sensitive issues as and when they arise, in a way appropriate to the age and understanding of their pupils.

I was going to talk about the importance of workplace and community relations, but the Division Bell is ringing. If I can sum up very briefly, we may be able to take our place in the Lobbies. We have invested greatly in affirmative approaches in terms of discouraging what can be a corrosive form of segregation while recognising the strength that exists within coherent and cohesive communities that bring ethnic groups together. On balance, I agree with the approach of my noble friend Lord Parekh that we have to develop a sense of shared Britishness. I agree also with the noble Baroness, Lady Ludford, in her desire to see integration rather than assimilation. Britishness is a diverse concept and we should celebrate it. We should work towards creating a shared sense of cultural norms and behaviour, particularly in relation to different faiths, cultural identities and different races. There lies the path for us in terms of a rational, sensible and sensitive approach to diversity within our society.

        Committee adjourned at twenty-five minutes before seven o'clock.

Written Statements

28 Nov 2005 : Column WS1
 

Global Health Security Initiative

The Minister of State, Department of Health (Lord Warner): My honourable friend the Minister of State (Rosie Winterton) has made the following Written Ministerial Statement.

On Friday 18 November, I attended the sixth ministerial forum of the Global Health Security Initiative (GHSI) in Rome, to agree actions to ensure a co-ordinated global response to health security issues, including preparing for an influenza pandemic.

Actions agreed at the meeting included:

    a commitment to compare national flu pandemic plans to share information and expertise, and to better understand different approaches to pandemic planning;

    a commitment to work with the World Health Organisation to develop and test protocols for early containment through a full range of public health interventions, including medical counter measures and equipment;

    a commitment to further develop risk communication tools to ensure decision makers and health professionals have consistent information internationally to help manage an influenza pandemic and ensure the public are kept fully informed of action to take in a pandemic situation;

    a commitment to develop, with WHO, approaches to improving capacity and access to vaccines; and

    a commitment to work together to prepare for the Beijing pledging conference in January 2006, and to have a clear outline of prioritised action to attract the funding for implementation by multinational organisations.

The United Kingdom has played an active role in developing and taking forward several areas of the GHSI's work programme. The GHSI was launched at a meeting of international health Ministers in Ottawa in 2001. Membership includes representatives of the UK, Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, Mexico and the United States together with the WHO and the European Commission for Health. The aims of the GHSI are to develop and implement co-operative, specialised public health protection measures in the event of a terrorist or natural incident.

In partnership with the USA, the UK has played a leading role in taking work forward on pandemic influenza. In June this year, the UK hosted a workshop on modelling pandemic spread and control strategies and an international tabletop exercise on preparedness and response. This event brought together modellers from each of the eight GHSI countries (Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan,
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column WS2
 
Mexico, the UK and the USA) to discuss potential response strategies at each stage of an emerging pandemic. Important work has also been undertaken to compare the pandemic influenza preparedness and response plans between countries to facilitate better planning and co-ordination.

At the meeting, Health Ministers agreed a programme of action to ensure that national pandemic planning continues to dovetail with international planning and that we communicate effectively both nationally and internationally. We recognised the importance of ensuring our populations and healthcare professionals have the high quality, consistent international information they need to manage the consequences of an outbreak of pandemic flu.

In addition, we agreed to share information and expertise to speed up the development and production of a pandemic flu vaccine. We also agreed to work together to boost global capacity for pandemic flu vaccine.

Together with Canada, the UK has also led on work in the area of risk management and communication. A 24/7 emergency contact network for emergency communications is in place and several successful international communication exercises have been undertaken.

A copy of the final Ministerial Statement from the Rome conference has been placed in the Library.

House of Lords: Writing Prohibition

The Chairman of Committees (Lord Brabazon of Tara): At its meeting last week, the Administration and Works Committee agreed that, from now on, the public should be permitted to take informal notes in the Public Gallery. As at present, the public will not be permitted to use electronic devices, and large or potentially dangerous items will have to be left in the cloakroom.

New admission cards, which reflect this change, have been ordered and will be brought into use as soon as practicable.

Transport Innovation Fund

Lord Davies of Oldham: My right honourable friend the Secretary of State for Transport (Alistair Darling) has made the following Ministerial Statement.

I am today announcing the names of seven local authorities which have bid successfully for funding to
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column WS3
 
develop innovative schemes to tackle congestion, including work on the feasibility of road pricing.

In July, I said that the way to the long-term goal of an effective road pricing system was through local or regional pilots. To support the development of such pilots, I said that we would make funding available, through the Transport Innovation Fund, to local authorities offering innovative local packages combining demand management and better public transport.

I announced on 5 July that up to £200 million per annum would be available from the fund for such schemes from 2008–09 to 2014–15—more if good schemes emerge.

Development of these schemes will be a complex undertaking. We therefore announced, also in July, that we would make available £18 million of pump-priming funding between 2005–06 and 2007–08 to support the work of local authorities in developing these local demand management schemes. I invited local authorities to submit bids for this development funding.

The response to this offer was extremely positive. We received 33 bids, many with high-quality ideas. The response to our invitation offers clear evidence that local authorities recognise that action planning to tackle congestion needs to start now.

I am today announcing pump-priming funding for seven areas to develop schemes:

    Bristol City Council, Bath and North East Somerset Council, North Somerset Council, South Gloucestershire Council;

    Cambridgeshire;

    Durham County Council (for Durham City);
     

28 Nov 2005 : Column WS4
 

    Greater Manchester;

    Shropshire County Council (for Shrewsbury);

    Tyne and Wear; and

    West Midlands conurbation.

The local authorities in all these areas will be carrying out feasibility studies on how local demand management and better public transport could together improve travelling conditions for their local people. Although no decisions have yet been taken by any of these authorities to adopt a road pricing solution to their congestion problems, all of them are planning to look at local road pricing as part of their consideration of demand management options. This work gives us the opportunity to develop practical design for road pricing schemes, within a coherent national pricing framework.

We will also be inviting officers from these authorities to work with us as members of the new Road Pricing Local Liaison Group together with others who have relevant experience of road-pricing matters. This group will be responsible for taking forward thinking on those aspects of road pricing where it makes sense to have a consistent approach across all schemes, including technical standards, design and scheme appraisal. Local authorities' input will be essential to this work.

Receipt of this pump-priming funding is no guarantee that an authority will be successful in bidding for the main Transport Innovation Fund. We will also work with other local authorities which have promising ideas for demand management, but which have not yet met the standard needed to secure development funding. We will be offering detailed feedback to all unsuccessful bidders, and currently plan to run a further round of pump-priming funding in 2006. I shall keep the House informed.

Written Answers

Monday, 28 November 2005.

Armed Forces Bill

Lord Astor of Hever asked Her Majesty's Government:

    Whether they intend to use the proposed Armed Forces Bill to reduce the competence of commanding officers to deal with alleged offences; and, if so, in what way; and [HL2399]

    Whether they intend to use the proposed Armed Forces Bill to remove from commanding officers the power to dismiss charges on matters within their competence; and [HL2400]

    Whether they intend to use the proposed Armed Forces Bill to confer on service prosecuting authorities the power to re-open a case dismissed by a commanding officer. [HL2401]

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Ministry of Defence (Lord Drayson): The Bill will provide for the offences which Army and Royal Air Force commanding officers may deal with summarily to be slightly extended, while there will be a reduction in the theoretically very wide summary jurisdiction of Royal Navy commanding officers. Our aim has been to harmonise the list of offences to include those which Royal Navy commanding officers can appropriately, and do in fact, deal with summarily at present. The Armed Forces Bill will also remove the power of commanding officers to dismiss a charge for any offence, without any form of hearing. Commanding officers will instead be able to discontinue, without a hearing, proceedings in any case which they can deal with summarily; unlike the present power of dismissal, this will not prevent further proceedings being brought within the service system.

Armed Forces: Service Law

Lord Astor of Hever asked Her Majesty's Government:

    What will be the legal status of The Manual of Military Law published by the Ministry of Defence, following the enactment of the proposed Armed Forces Bill. [HL2397]

Lord Drayson: In common with the present manuals of naval, military and air force law, the new manual of service law will be a reference document with no legal status.

British Passports

Lord Avebury asked Her Majesty's Government:

    Why the Passport Service has no mechanism for dealing with applications from British citizens who, having lived abroad for a number of years, need to apply for replacement of a lost or stolen passport in the United Kingdom, but have access to no person who is qualified to countersign the application. [HL2347]

The Minister of State, Home Office (Baroness Scotland of Asthal): A person is qualified to countersign a passport application if he or she is a professional person or person of standing in the community who holds a current British or Irish passport, is not related to or in a relationship with the applicant and has known the applicant for two years or more. A countersignature is needed for first passport applications and applications to replace lost or stolen passports. When a British citizen who has recently arrived in the UK after living abroad applies to replace a lost or stolen passport, and in other exceptional circumstances, the UK Passport Service will consider an application supported by a countersignatory who has known the applicant for less than two years but otherwise meets the criteria.

This will secure a level playing field between maintained and PVI providers, ensuring, for the first time, that all children receive the same entitlement regardless of the setting their parents chose. Citizens for Europe Programme

Lord Willoughby de Broke asked Her Majesty's Government:

    What is the purpose of the Citizens for Europe Programme; when it was established and under what authority; who are the members of its council; what is its budget; and how often it meets. [HL2300]

Lord Davies of Oldham: The Citizens for Europe Programme is a proposed EU funding programme which would run from 2007 to 2013. It will replace the current programme to promote active European citizenship (civic participation) which began in 2004. The programme is still being negotiated by the Council of Ministers and the European Parliament and therefore its content has not yet been agreed. However, the proposed overarching objectives of the programme are to give citizens the opportunity to interact; to develop a sense of European identity based on common values; and to celebrate the diversity of our cultures. Citizens will be targeted both directly and via civil society organisations. The programme will be established under Articles 151 and 308 of the EC treaty. The Commission has proposed a budget of €235 million (£159 million). However, a decision on the programme's actual budget will not be made until an agreement has been reached on the EU budget as a whole for the next financial perspective.

If adopted, the programme would not have a council but it would probably have a management committee. The Commission proposes that such a committee should decide implementation arrangements for the programme. These might include the selection procedures and criteria for each call for proposals; and procedures for monitoring and evaluating the programme.

Counter-terrorism

Lord Jopling asked Her Majesty's Government:

28 Nov 2005 : Column WA5
 

Baroness Scotland of Asthal: The Anti-terrorism, Crime and Security Act 2001 contains measures to improve the security of dangerous substances that may be targeted or used by terrorists. Schedule 5 of that Act lists those pathogens and toxins that potentially pose the greatest risk to human life if misused by terrorists. These substances are subject to the controls set out in the Act. The provisions set out in Part 7 of the ATCS Act place an obligation on managers of laboratories and other premises holding stocks of specified disease causing pathogens and toxins to notify their holdings and to comply with any reasonable security requirements which the police may impose. The provisions within counter-terrorism legislation are kept under constant review.

Prisons: Juvenile Detention

Lord Roberts of Llandudno asked Her Majesty's Government:

    How many under-18 year-olds were imprisoned in prisons for adults in the United Kingdom in 1980, 1990, 1995, 2000 and 2004. [HL1816]

Baroness Scotland of Asthal: The answers for 2000 and 2004 are in the tables below. It has not been possible to provide figures for 1980, 1990 and 1995.

* These prison establishments hold a mixture of juvenile and adults within the same perimeter. However, since the creation of the Youth Justice Board in 2000, male juveniles are held in separate units and do not mix with adults. It is not usual to hold juvenile males at Chelmsford. However, there is provision within the service level agreement between the Prison Service and YJB to hold them outside normal juvenile accommodation for

exceptional circumstance—for example, in that nearest to court for court appearances. Prior to April 2000, juveniles were held within the secure estate, but were kept separate from the adult population. Following the creation of the YJB in April 2000, there has been a phased withdrawal of under-18 year-olds from the general secure estate into the dedicated juvenile estate.

Northern Ireland 30 June 2000

*** All prisoners associated with Maghaberry in the tables are actually females. Female prisoners were housed in a separate wing of Maghaberry until July 2004.


1 Maghaberry Prison: A high-security prison housing adult male long-term sentenced and remand prisoners, both in separated and integrated conditions. Maghaberry also has responsibility for male immigration detainees who are accommodated in its facility at Belfast.


2 Magilligan Prison: A medium-security prison housing shorter-term sentenced adult male prisoners which also has low security accommodation for selected prisoners.


3 Hydebank Wood: A young offenders centre and prison for male remands and sentenced young offenders between the ages of 17 and 21 and all female prisoners including young offenders and female immigration detainees.






Radcliffe Rules

Lord Lester of Herne Hill asked Her Majesty's Government:

    Further to the reply by the Lord Bassam of Brighton on 17 November (Official Report, col. 1187), whether the Radcliffe rules should apply to Ministers as well as to the Civil Service and the Diplomatic Service; and, if not, what is the reason for the difference of treatment as regards the publication of memoirs. [HL2442]
     

28 Nov 2005 : Column WA12
 

Lord Bassam of Brighton: The Radcliffe rules apply to ministerial memoirs and were established as a result of the report of the Committee of Privy Counsellors on Ministerial Memoirs, chaired by Lord Radcliffe and published in 1976. The Radcliffe principles also apply to civil servants, including special advisers, and members of the Diplomatic Service.

Royal Irish Regiment: Home Service Battalions

Lord Maginnis of Drumglass asked Her Majesty's Government:

    Further to the Written Answer by the Lord Drayson on 10 November (WA 110), why, in light of the Army's duty of care towards its soldiers, there is no information held on the hours worked by full-time soldiers from each of the Home Service battalions of the Royal Irish Regiment. [HL2293]

Lord Drayson: Working time regulations apply to the Armed Forces but, in recognition of the overriding need to maintain the operational effectiveness of the Armed Forces, exemptions have been granted, including a specific exemption from the requirement to record working hours. Commanders are, however, directed to be aware of the impact of any specific order on the working hours of their subordinates.

Royal Irish Regiment: Uniforms

Lord Laird asked Her Majesty's Government:

    Whether they gave guidance to members of the Royal Irish Regiment concerning the wearing of their uniforms at Remembrance Day occasions; and, if so, what was the nature of such guidance; why it was issued; and how the guidance has changed over each of the past five years. [HL2308]

Lord Drayson: Permission to wear uniform for an event, whether an act of remembrance or other formal occasions such as weddings, is granted through the commanding officer of each battalion. The event is "risk assessed" and permission granted or not granted accordingly. This policy has not changed in the past five years.

Smallpox Vaccination: Medical Staff

Lord Jopling asked Her Majesty's Government:

    Further to the Written Answer by the Lord Warner on 7 September 2004 (WA 137), how many (a) doctors; (b) nurses; (c) ambulance staff; (d) scientists; and (e) others have been vaccinated for smallpox so as to enable them to deal with a terrorist attack; and how many of these have had complications leading to further treatment using vaccine immunoglobulin. [HL2369]
     

28 Nov 2005 : Column WA13
 

Lord Warner: At 18 November 2005, a total of 516 people in the United Kingdom had been vaccinated against smallpox as part of our contingency planning. Of these 147 were doctors, 164 were nurses, 100 were ambulance staff, 32 were scientists and 73 held other related healthcare occupations.

None of these had adverse complications that required vaccinia immunoglobulin.

Lord Jopling asked Her Majesty's Government:

    Further to the Written Answer by the Lord Warner on 18 November 2004 (WA 219), how many doses of undiluted smallpox vaccines are held in the government stockpile; and how many doses of vaccine immunoglobulin are held or due for delivery. [HL2370]

Lord Warner: The United Kingdom Government have sufficient smallpox vaccine to mount a mass vaccination campaign of the whole population.
 
28 Nov 2005 : Column WA14
 

More than 10,000 doses of vaccina immunoglobulin have been ordered sufficient to treat those who may develop severe side effects as a result of vaccination in a mass vaccination campaign and also to protect those at increased risk, as a result of contact with cases, who cannot receive the vaccine alone. The order was placed in June for delivery during the next 12 months.

Weights and Measures (Miscellaneous Foods) (Amendment) Order 2005

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts asked Her Majesty's Government:

    Whether, as a result of the provisions of the Weights and Measures (Miscellaneous Foods) (Amendment) Order 2005 (S.I. 2005/3057), pre-packed food may now be marketed without the weight being clearly marked on each saleable unit. [HL2491]

Lord Sainsbury of Turville: No. The order does not affect existing requirements in UK legislation that the nominal quantity be marked on packaged goods.
